        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>There are a number of good minor-league hitters who play catcher. Many fewer of them play catcher well enough to actually succeed at the position in the majors. For those among us who lack the expertise to identify the capable defenders, the prospect of reaching firm conclusions about minor-league receivers poses some difficulty. In the case of Maxwell, however, all the objective indicators are positive. According to the methodologies employed by Baseball Prospectus and Clay Davenport, Maxwell has mostly acquitted himself as an above-average defender in the minors. According to the catcher-framing metrics used by Baseball Prospectus and StatCorner, respectively, Maxwell's framing ability was in the average range during his brief tour of the majors last year. The defense oughtn't be an issue, is the point. The most pressing issue for Maxwell's 2017 campaign is probably the duo of Stephen Vogt and Josh Phegley, whose presence is likely to relegate Maxwell to the minors at the start of the season. Given a chance, though, Maxwell appears to possess enough offensive and defensive ability to profile as an average major leaguer. (Carson Cistulli)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Given a chance, Maxwell appears to possess enough offensive and defensive ability to profile as an average major league. His opportunities for playing time are correlated inversely with the health and effectiveness ofStephen Vogt and Josh Phegley.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
